The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy should be administered only for the preservation of health and with hope of benefit and caution against the occurrence of illness, and that it should be performed in one of the veins which are on the elbow, namely the cephalic, dark, and basilica veins; and that it should be performed at the beginning of spring, after fasting in spring and autumn, and near meals in winter when there are signs of repletion.

"But in that body where there is much blood already scorched and it is sharp and causes fever to occur, one must draw out blood from him in one session, and let much of the drawn blood be, and let the aperture be widened until fainting occurs. And let your hand be placed over his pulse during the drawing of the blood, so that death does not happen instead of fainting, for many times this happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent."
"And when you wish to loosen the arm and evacuate blood a second time, since the orifice of the vein is already closed and it is difficult to draw out blood, then press there with force and let him extend his arm gently, for this is very bad."
"Let him leave it until it is phlebotomized again; or remove that with the sharpness of the phlebotomy which has coagulated blood in the orifice of the vein; or place there tyrian aloe, or sagazena, and press there gently until the blood flows out."
"And if the vein is inflamed, then leave that alone and do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided."
"And if there is cause for you to provoke evacuation of blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then let him be phlebotomized on that spot or on the other arm in another vein."
"And the vein called 'the rope of the arm' is phlebotomized in place of the furcula or the basilica when they are not found or are hidden, for it is composed of those."
"And the quality of phlebotomy of him is that the patient should place his hand in cold water until the vein becomes engorged and appears manifestly."
"And let him be strengthened on that with a linen bandage with moderate tension; and let phlebotomy be done transversely, not lengthwise nor according to the length."
"And let the aperture be wide and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize him on the junction of the hand a little."
"And if blood flow is prohibited, then immerse your hand in cold water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your purpose."
"But if it's summer and you're excused from immersing your hand in cold water, then place a cloth soaked in cold water on the body to slow down blood flow during winter."
"And the flebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, because the patient has no palpable pulse or nerves."
"And phlebotomy of the little vein on the right hand's wrist is beneficial for liver ailments."
"And the quality of phlebotomy is that you strengthen the hand by rubbing it with a linen cloth or your hand, after immersing it in cold water until the vein is inflated and clearly visible; then transverse flebotomy should be performed."
"And remember not to rub your hand too much during phlebotomy, as the nerves are exposed beneath the skin."
"And after immersing the hand in cold water, let the blood flow into that vessel, because if you don't immerse it in cold water, the blood will coagulate at the opening of the vein and prevent its flow."
"And once the blood has been extracted according to what is needed, you should then press over the vein with oil and salt so that it does not heal too quickly; similarly, do this for any other branch."
"But the benefit of phlebotomy from that vein on the left hand is that it is beneficial for stomach ailments."
"And do likewise in performing phlebotomy on this one as you did on the other."
"And the benefit of phlebotomy from the saphenous vein is for ailments that are below the body's surface, such as those relating to the womb, and retention of menses, and for ailments of the skin like scabies and ulcers on old wounds, and their similar ailments."
"And the quality of phlebotomy for this [person] is that the patient should immerse their foot in cold water and rub it until the veins appear clearly; then tie a bandage around the junction of the foot with a cord; and the location of the vein is evident near the ankle, and from there it branches out into several smaller branches on the surface of the foot."
"And therefore perform phlebotomy on one of the larger branches or below the ankle near its junction with the leg, because it is better and more salubrious."
"And if you perform phlebotomy on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves that lie beneath it and make your incision at an angle, as if you were cutting across it."
"And may God grant you skill in performing phlebotomy."
"But if bleeding is prohibited, then immerse the patient's foot in cold water and let the blood flow into that vessel until it is evacuated."
"And if the phlebotomist makes an error on the first attempt, then make another incision nearby because that location is safe; do not fear causing harm, but rather beware of the nerves as we have said."
"And similarly perform phlebotomy on the other foot with caution."
"And concerning the sciatic vein, its location is as we have said: near the ankle and behind the inner ankle bone."
"When performing phlebotomy on them, it causes hip pain due to bloodletting."
"The quality of the phlebotomy on them is that the patient enters into a bath, and quickly and strongly presses their thigh flesh on the inner side above the heel with four such cloths placed one by one using a long subtle bandage so that only this appears."
"Therefore, when it appears, you should perform phlebotomy according to each person's ability and according to their transverse vein, if possible, or else perform incision or sectioning according to the location."
"And it is common in many men but not harmful. And if you cannot find that one, perform phlebotomy on another branch of the vein that appears at the back of the ear facing the little finger."
"And beware of nerves, and let flow as much blood as you desire."
"Then untie the ligature and pause on the location of the phlebotomy until it stops bleeding, because it heals easily."
"And regarding suction and the quality of using them."
"Suctions are made of horn, bronze, or glass."
"And suction is done in two ways: one with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other without scarification."
"And this suction which is called scarification, is done in two ways: either with fire or without fire."
"And the places on the body where suction is administered with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen in number, of which one is placed at the forehead, which is the last part of the head; another is midway between two parts of the neck; suction on the chin below the jawbone but above the mouth; suction on both shoulders; suction over the hip joint, which is near the buttocks; suction on both thighs."
"And bloodletting does not remove the blood from large veins, but only from small ones scattered throughout the flesh, so that virtue is not lost through it as happens with flebotomy; and it is not necessary for bloodletting to be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused bypletion until the entire body has been evacuated."
"But if bloodletting is provoked by illness or by continualpletion, we administer it at any hour at the beginning and end of the month, and in the middle of it, and at any other time."
"And therefore when there is an increase in any of these causes, especially if it occurs in the third or middle of the month, we order bloodletting to be done three days or two days after that time."
"And the benefit of bloodletting from the neck is that when it causes heaviness in the head and dizziness, it must be evacuated appropriately throughout the entire body."
"And this bloodletting is done at the location of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein."
"But it is not allowed to use this method in those who have a fat constitution, or in those with catarrh, because it causes great harm."
"And therefore, it should not be used for the elderly, and for those who have illnesses caused by heat."
"But those who are continually subjected to it will suffer from forgetfulness. And therefore, it is necessary that you command the bloodletter not to press his hand too much on the vein at the beginning, so as to avoid generating forgetfulness."
"But bloodletting of the right arm is done at the location of phlebotomy of the black vein and the basilica vein: and therefore it causes asthma, constriction of the lung, splitting of the windpipe, cough, andpletion."
"And therefore, it is necessary that bloodletting from the right arm be done carefully, because if it is too deep it generates weakness in the body and stomach."
"And bloodletting from the left arm causes accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia, migraine, fever, and toothache. And it is done at the location of phlebotomy of the basilica vein."
"And you must command the bloodletter not to press his hand on the scarification lest he should wound the artery and cause a flow of blood."
"And bloodletting from the left cheek causes coldness in the mouth, gum disease, and signs of diseases that occur in the mouth. And it is done at the location of phlebotomy of the arteria helicalis which are on both lips."
"But bloodletting that is done above the two breasts causes catarrh, which occurs due topletion of heat."
"And bloodletting from the two divisions of the thighs causes benefit from phlebotomy of three veins: the basilica, black and cephalic veins. Because it attracts blood from all those veins that are in the flesh, as well as the small veins of other larger veins, so that the attraction can reach to the three veins."
"And command the bloodletter not to proceed with scarification, because the location is devoid of flesh and has nerves and arteries nearby."
"And it is a bloodletting done on the hip, which causes hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcers nearby."
"And you must command the bloodletter to make a large bloodletting using a lancet because the location requires a strong section. And that the veins should be strongly pulled and then scarified with scarification."
"But bloodletting from the camber minusso reduces gradually through minute increments. Because it attracts blood from the entire body and causes ancient pains of the lungs, uterus, and bladder. And it provokes menstruation, and causes bothersome diseases. And it is done at the location of phlebotomy of two sophena veins, alahorcoben vein, and two cavernous veins."
"However, they debilitate the entire body and cause fainting in many people."
"And the benefit of bloodletting from the left armpit is similar to that of bloodletting from both arms."
"The quality of the position of bloodletting is that at the beginning it should be shallow and then proceed gradually by suction. And do not prolong the duration of bloodletting, but rather pause it lightly and remove it gently so that the humors come to the location by gentle arrival." "And do not cease to repeat this until you see the location becoming reddened and swollen, and that the color of blood appears."
"Then scarify again and repeat suction little by little, and pay attention to the condition of the patient's body."
"Because that person who has tender skin with many pores, you must scarify him once and not more, lest the location become ulcerated."
"And command the bloodletter to widen the scarification a little and then proceed with gentle suction."
"And if the blood is thickened, then you must scarify twice because in the first scarification the path of subtle attraction to the blood and humidity of it becomes open." "But in the second one, the extraction of thick blood is acquired through examination."
"And if the blood is very dark-colored, then a third scarification should be made to complete it."
"Finally, when we want to extract blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is sufficient."
"And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we should make the scarification large. And if we know that the blood is thick, then we must scarify by a deep scarification."
"The term 'scarification' refers to the depth of the cut as far as the skin only."
"And if the skin of someone is thick, hard, dark, and their pores are narrow, then the locations for bloodletting should be anointed with gentle, softening, resolving oils; if it is in summer time, such as oil of aloe or violet oil, or doughnut oil, or celery seed oil. And if it is in wintertime, then use narcissus oil or lily oil or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and so on."
"If excessive humor is thick and cold, then oil of marjoram, or oil of balsam, or oil of aloe, or oil of aneth should be applied."
"If the patient is of tender flesh and wide pores, then oil should be prohibited, and the places around the wound should be washed with hot water or warm water infused with salt, or with decoction of wormwood, or with decoction of borrage, or similar remedies."
"And for one whose blood is excessively moist, wash their wounds with sour wine, or water of myrtle and sumac, and similar remedies."
"And for one whose humors are thick, wash their wounds with old wine, or water of hyssop (majoram), or decoction of dill, or chamomile, and similar remedies."
"And the wound should be covered before bathing. It is necessary to apply it near the bath for one or two hours after bathing, and to sleep near the wound."
"What needs to happen in order to regulate the bleeding from a vein that has been opened before cauterization?"
"Consider this at the outset: If the patient or one to be bled is choleric and full of hot and inflamed blood, then one should take causes for cooling, such as sour grapes, endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secanabin, and julep, and their like."
"And pauses from their foods such as chickens, birds' flesh, roasted meats, and dishes seasoned with spices, and similar things."
"And for one whose temperament is phlegmatic, it is necessary to give them to drink syrup of almonds or syrup of hyssop with fennel seeds and sumac, and odoriferous wine between old and new; and that they be advised to use moderation in drinking and eating, and avoid foods such as chickens, canary birds, pheasants, small birds, and pigeons."
"And on the day of venesection or phlebotomy, let drinking be greater than eating."
"And let some men be given to drink tyriaca alpharac, or musk medicines, such as muscata, or alcelita before venesection or phlebotomy or nearby, so that the principal members may be comforted and the blood subtilized; and let them not take that medicine afterward."
"But let those venesections that are not made for scarring be made in such a way as to pause from the liver, and on the spleen and podagra (gout), and on the navel, in the locations of the joints, and the armpits of the arms, because these members do not suffer scarification upon them."
"And they are not sought except for attraction of blood from the member, thus as venesection pause on the breast in illness of flow of blood from the nose; and they use that for the dissolution of viscous phlegm from the member, thus as pausing on the belly and the navel; because it rarifies the member and scatters it and removes pain by resolving phlegm."
"And already venesection is made on the temples when they happen in their apoplexy or palsy, because by virtue of strong attraction it draws out the phlegm and expels the wind from its location."
"And similarly it does when venesection is made on the liver and spleen for resolving phlegm in those enclosed."
"And these venesections are administered solely with suction, and sometimes they are administered with fire."
"And sometimes they are administered full of water from the Tiber in illnesses of the spleen."
"This is so that the venesection may be full and larger, so it is water boiled alone or water in which herbs are cooked for this purpose."
"Form of venesection administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two finger-breadths according to how we have formed it; and let the quantity of pitch be the most of a palm; and let it be on the side of it, in the location that is in the circuit, the most of it, and let the wick be small according to the quantity that enters into it through a needle."
"Let it be made of wormwood or citron, of large margins, lion's tooth, purslane, and hyssop, for that they do not harm the member by their position over it."
"And let it be in the middle of it a transverse wick of wormwood or iron, where the candle is paused with fire."
"And already this venesection is much greater than that which we have recommended; and the little one is less according to the illnesses and the state of him who uses it."
"And the venesections of the old and weakened are different from those of the young and robust ones."
"And the quality of pausing this venesection with fire on the member is that it extends the wick with fire of decent wood before a small candle of wax, and pauses that on the middle of the transverse wick in the middle location of the venesection, for that the fire may be kindled around the parting of the veins so that not the body of the sick person may be burned."
"And approach should be paused on the member of the sick person, and let it be over the wick that we have said, until when we have the venesections and do not need to hold the candle and that smoke rises above that wick, then the venesection will soon be dissolved."
"And approach the wick according to its manner, and repeat it if necessary."
"More in venesection that is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse wick nor wick, and it is not administered unless it be filled with water, and it is paused only on the member."
"And this is its form: And this venesection, the greater it is, the better, for that it can receive a great quantity of water."
"Chapter XCVI."
"Bloodletting is not administered in most members but in those where it is impossible to place venesections due to their smallness, such as the eye and gum and similar ones; or in member devoid of flesh, such as the toe and nose and their like."
"And the quality of using those bloodsuckers that are found in muddy waters is if they are washed clean."
"And approach they should be laid day and night in muddy water until they are hungry, and that no cause remains in the belly of those; and approach let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and venesections; and approach rub the member of the sick person until it reddens; and approach they should be paused on it."
"And when they are full and satiated."
"And if it is possible to suck from the location with venesection, it is more preferable in treatment."
"But if not, then wash the location with sour wine and approach with much water; and let it be rubbed and expressed."
"And if the body of blood is prolonged near the skin of the bloodsuckers and it is hot, then take a cloth of linen in warm water and pause that over until the heat is absorbed."
"And if the blood is multiplied, then sprinkle over it three-times cooked vitriol, or walnuts and similar ones of the causes astringent, until the blood be arrested; or let the most of roasted chickpeas be paused on the location, and let them be laid until the chickpeas adhere to the location, and the blood is arrested."
"And if necessary, iteration of bloodsuckers, when it is possible to change those."
"And if bloodsuckers are prohibited from suspension, then let the location be rubbed with fresh blood or put a needle in the locations until blood comes out; and approach they should be paused, for those when they feel some blood soon adhere."
"And when you want them to let go, sprinkle over those a little of thrice-cooked garlic, or salt, or ashes, and they will soon let go."
"So begins the prologue of the third part: of restoration." "This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine, it is the restoration of fracture and accidental dislocation in the bones."
"Oh, son, know that they boast of this chapter the foolish physicians and the common ones and those who at any time do not regard the books of the Ancients nor read a letter of theirs."
"And for this reason it happens that this manner of the sciences in our land is private; and I am not he who has forgotten at any time be all; and have not added what follows, except for the length of reading the books of the Ancients and study on the understanding of theirs, until extracted the science from them."
"And now I turn to experimentation and study throughout my life."
"And now I have already described in this chapter everything that pertains to my knowledge and on which experiments have been conducted; for the sake of brevity, I have shortened it for you and relieved you from lengthiness; and shortened it even further by abbreviation; and shown it plainly enough in declaration; and formed many forms of instruments administered therein, as is done by addition in declaration, just as I have done in two preceding chapters."
"Chapter one."
"Summary and collections from the consideration of fractures of bones, from which a preliminary explanation is necessary before we begin to remember the members that are broken and diverse if any." "Therefore I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of speech, and chapters at the understanding of which at the beginning the artisans are forced to stand still and deliberate about the truth of those; so it is you and those who are eager that know or learn this noble art other than you."
"And then I say that when some fracture or dislocation or torsion or compression occurs, immediately you should take flebotomy on the patient or let him bleed, or whatever ensemble, if there is no prohibition of any cause, such as debility of strength, or if it is a child to whom it has happened, or an old person, or during a time of great heat, or cold."
"And it is appropriate that he should be content with his diet of fresh herbs and of the flesh of birds and crabs; and let him avoid heavy meats and filling foods, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of an abscess cautery, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the member."
"And then return the patient to his usual first regimen, with which he is accustomed to live."
"And then when the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be nourished with nutritious foods of thick and heavy nourishment in which there is viscosity, such as pastries made from wheat cooked with water, rice, milk, chicken, beef, fresh cheese, thick wine and similar ones, and with this regimen coagulation of fracture occurs more easily and better."
"And know that the bones of the elderly when they are in men who are in the first decline and in the decrepit, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the dryness of the members of theirs."
"But nevertheless nature is ordered and adapted over the broken bone of any part with some cause such as glue in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it until it is conglutinated and united together, until the end of fortitude and firmness comes, like fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits that from its works."
"And for this reason it is necessary to pause the patient's foods, foods in which there is gravity and viscosity and thickness, as we have said."
"And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves."
"And I come with remembrance of each species of fracture, explained in its own chapter separated from the others."
"Great diversity even within itself." "And the species of bone fracture are diversified within themselves; for there is an arched fracture, other than that which occurs in stars; and sometimes there is a fracture according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the satagia and astellas and additional ones that proceed and do not proceed, such as is made in the pen when it is prepared to write, and it is a fracture with swelling and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture."
"And for each species there is an appropriate treatment in the restoration of it, which will come to remembrance later in its separate place."
"Many signs of bone fracture." "And for this reason a bone fracture is known: there is tortuosity of it, and prominence and palpation of it to the touch, and that when you compress it with your hand."
"And then when there is no apparent tortuosity in the place nor is it felt when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then it is not here a fracture."
"It may happen that there is tortuosity or a small fissure, in which case you should not move it with your hand and compress all of it; rather pause over it with the medicines of which remembrance will be given at that appropriate moment for the place; and especially it should be stretched by subtle straitening."
"And know that the place where it is arched and not split in two or other than that which happens in the satagia, when all the part of its comparison has been removed, then you should begin from your hour to rectify it having cauterization done upon it."
"And if cauterization is applied to it, then leave it for a few days until the cautery has ceased; and meanwhile treat it according to each manner that you can of elevation and application."
"And know that the restoration and treatment of it is easier than for the bone in which satagie has happened."
"And especially strengthen it according to what will come to remembrance later."
"But if there are satagia in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from two parts, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand."
"And let your position be such that you hold the member in its natural figure while the parts of the broken member are extended, then you should strive to return those additions with treatment and according to each manner that you can in their places."
"And guard yourself according to your skill that no harm comes upon the patient by your operation of them nor injury; and strive to apply one bone to its companion with the best forms you can."
"And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you touch it with your hand and if here there is any different cause, then correct it and treat it according to the quantity of your virtue."
"And avoid violent extension and compression, as many foolish doctors do who often cause cautery to be applied because of their operation, or cause contraction in the member as we have seen from the operation of many."
The quality of the restriction in the broken limb is that which I recommend; so you should know that broken limbs are differentiated by their smallness and greatness and by their shape.
And for those that are small like the arm and the hand and foot, then it is necessary that their dressing should be of very fine linen.
But those that are large like the thigh or the leg, there should be a wrapping of linen and hard material; for this tight binding connects the large member and greatly strengthens it from all sides with restriction, ensuring that nothing enters.
And so beginning, so your bandaging of the fracture should be done carefully with proper binding applied there, over the place of that fracture with three wrappings or four according to what the limb deserves; and tighten it well with your hand using the linen.
And then take the remaining part above the place of the fracture and tighten it even more than before with your binding to ensure that there is not too much restriction at the site of the fracture. And then extend yourself with wrapping around the place of the fracture somewhat, and slowly loosen the binding little by little until you take one large part from it.
And then take another bandage and wrap it around that other time over the place of the fracture with wrapping, and then go with the wrappings below the fracture; and let your operation be in tightening the wrapping and loosening it according to what has been said about the first wrapping above.
And then pause between the wrappings with a piece of linen or cloth with which to wrap around the tortuosity of the fracture if there is any tortuosity there; and if not, do not pause in it for any cause.
And then place another bandage over it, and then wrap around these wrappings gently and evenly at once if there is no inflammation or pus there, and afterwards pause over that which sits on the pus or inflammation; and leave it for a few days; and then tighten over it with stitches.
And let the stitches be of mature cane, carefully made; or of oak wood; or of branches of palm tree; and let the width of each stitch be according to this shape and figure:
But it is necessary that the stitch which is placed over that fracture be thicker and wider than the other stitches, and the length of the stitch according to what is measured on the limb in magnitude and smallness; and then tighten over the stitches with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then lay over it with linen gently as has been said about tightening, and let your tightening be according to the place of the fracture more, and the further away from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and be of wool; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish people doing when placing their threads of twisted cords, it is a great error, for when the tightening is too much, equality will not be obtained.
And also very fine threads are not convenient because with them you will not be able to tighten as much as you want, and there should not be less than one finger's breadth between stitch and stitch.
And if the illness is said to be caused by the ends of the stitches near the tightening in the raw places, then pause those with a piece of linen or woolen cloth between them so that it does not cause injury.
Sometimes fracture occurs with plague, and disruption happens in the tendon, and I have written about that one in the chapter according to its particularity.
And know that not all broken limbs should be tightened with stitches on the first day; for which reason is that when a limb is large, it does not require tightening with stitches until after 2 or 3 or more days, according to your certainty of the arrival of pus.
And this is the manner of the bandages with which the Ancients restored fractures and dislocations and twists.
"Method of plastering as a remedy for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many complex cases and particularly for women and children, because it does not cause heat nor friction: take some flour powder, specifically the bran left after removing it from the walls of the mill."
"And if you find this too thick and coarse, sieve it through a clean cloth, then use the finer flour for your dough."
"Another method of plastering for the restoration of fractures, dislocations and wry necks: take assafetida, laudanum, acacia, enula, and majoram, which is the root of wild celery, of each one dram; myrrh and aloe of each five drams; alochil of twenty drams. All these should be finely ground and sifted, then mixed with white of egg or flour paste if the patient's skin is scorched. This plaster has a good composition and restores lightly broken bones, suitable for many people."
"Another method of plaster for the treatment of fractures and wry necks: take majoram, white lead, myrrh, aloe, acacia and gum arabic in equal amounts, each one dram."
"All of these are finely ground in a mortar with a wooden pestle and sifted, then mixed with water or flour paste and administered."
"A method of plaster for joints and separated bones in their places, which causes pain accidentally in those areas and also for fissures and fractures: take wool without grease, immerse it in vinegar, olive oil and honey, then place it on the affected area."
"Note that this plaster does not have virtue for restoration but serves to draw out pus, cauterize and expel pains properly."
"Another method of plaster that restores broken bones: take fig leaves and poppy leaves, both together make a plaster of them."
"A brief method of plaster administration for the restoration of bone fractures and when you want to resolve the remaining pus: take white lead, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, bran and flour, all together one part each; all are finely ground and mixed with wine if the member is not scorched."
"But if it is scorched, then use that with water of fresh coriander or simply water and flour paste."
"Another method of plaster which is very strong in resolving [pus], better than the first one, when a hard boil occurs during the restoration of the member: take white lead and its seed, balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, flour and bran all together one part each; all are finely ground and mixed with water of soapwort or sweet water or wine, depending on the quality of the member and the relaxation of its color."
"But Hippocrates in his book does not specify that it should be placed on the broken member during its restoration, but rather cerote made from wax and oil, and nothing else; and he recommends that it should have a consistency between thickness and subtlety."
"But it is seen in Galen that they should be placed on the broken member during its restoration and the causes of dryness with little heat are such as myrrh, incense and similar things to those causes that we have recommended."
"A method of plaster that relieves injury and pain: take gum, wax, human hair cuttings or feathers, white lead, salt all together one part each; finely ground and sifted, mix it to make a plaster."
"But the quantity according to how much the plaster should remain on it and appropriately be removed is this: if there is no pain or swelling and the bone is not broken, then you do not remove it for many days." "And if severe pain or swelling or inflammation occurs at the place, then you remove it immediately or in that hour, and replace the plaster on it."
"And take a cloth of it and a damp sea sponge, and immerse that in hot water, and with that wash the affected area inside until the swelling subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the member rest for an hour. Then place on it lanolin immersed in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and leave it on overnight until you are sure of a cauterized boil, and that the inflammation of the member has abated and the pain of it has been removed."
"And then reapply that subtle bandage and plaster gently and do not tighten too much with your first constraint, and subtly with it until it is healed."
"And if at times the boil and the heat and pain and inflammation and all accidents are already removed from everything, and you need plaster and bandaging, then apply them again in the same way as you did at the beginning."
"And if no cause of those that we have mentioned occurs to the member, then do not remove it but let it remain for three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days according to how you see it in the removal of the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and approaches the coagulation of the flesh on it."
"And then adjust the bandage more than your first one completely, and adjust it to the thickness of the food of the sick person, according to how much is preceded by its restoration."
"But if you see that the place of the fracture is already dry and shriveled more than it should be, then know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore immerse it in hot water every time you remove it; do this for all three days. And loosen the bandage a little, and by this operation blood reaches the member, and it heals easily."
"But what foolish restorers of the fracture of the member do at another time when it is not restored as it should be and is restored crookedly, that is an error according to their operation, and great fear, and if it were straightener then it would have increased the ancients in their books and guarded against this."
"And I have not seen any of them do that, therefore it is more reasonable that they should not do it."
"Chapter II: On fracture when it occurs in the head."
"Fractures have various shapes and causes. Some are caused by blows that break all the bone into its cavity, like a broken stick, and these are called 'ascial'. Others are caused when a blow cuts part of the bone without reaching the marrow, and these are called 'levatio absolute'."
"The plague of these two fractures is either great or small. The fracture that is crushed or bruised results from a blow with force or fall on stone."
"This fracture is penetrating and approaches the periosteum of the bone or is located on the surface of the bone. The severity of these two fractures can be great or small."
"And there is a fracture that occurs within the bone itself, in its finest detail, like a hair's width; this type of fracture is called 'capillary fracture'."
"And there is also fracture that occurs due to falls or blows from stone or similar objects, which causes fragments of bone to be driven into the interior of the bone itself, particularly in concave areas like those found at the elbow joint when it suffers a blow and becomes fractured."
"And this happens more often in the bones of damp places, as is seen in the heads of infants."
"Therefore, all these species of fractures are painful like stars separated by intervals." "And I have come to remember all this in its proper place."
"I know all these species of fractures through detection on those bones and inquiry by touching and observing the rotten flesh that is on them."
"A capillary fracture is recognized because when it is exposed on the bone and cleaned, it appears black to the touch."
"But in the treatment of fractures, one should first consider the accompanying conditions of the illness. If the patient has symptoms such as vomiting from cholera, syncope, loss of consciousness, fever, pallor and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms to those found in serious conditions, it is not advisable to attempt a cure."
"However, if the patient does not have any accompanying symptoms, then you may begin treating the fracture."
"The reason for this is that if the fracture has already begun to set before you have attended to it, particularly during the cold days of winter, then you should study the bone tissue in detail on the fourteenth day according to all its dispositions."
"But if it is during the summer days, then you should expose the bone on the seventh day, ensuring that what remains of the bone tissue has become corrupted by the surrounding flesh, and that the symptoms we have mentioned occur."
"If the fracture has already reached the skin covering the brain and is in many small fragments and adherent, then you should incise the comminuted and adherent fragment, following my instructions."
"Therefore, you should carefully shave the patient's head and uncover the bone according to your custom and manner as best you can, following the appearance of the fracture, so that it is light upon the patient."
"If it happens that you must make a detection of the bone, with blood flowing or a hot abscess, then you should postpone the operation until what is necessary has been done. And this is how: you should dress the wound with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and allow the fracture to rest until the abscess is sedated and you are sure that there is no longer any bleeding. Then begin with the circumcision of the bone and the ablation of it."
"This is according to one of two operative methods."
"The first method is that you should incise the bone with a straight incision, using an instrument such as this: ... And then use another instrument of similar shape but slightly wider..."
"Then use an even wider instrument."
"You should have a variety of instruments available to you, some wider than others, and some shorter than others. They should be sharp at their tips and made of iron or bronze."
"Use them with ease in percussion on the incision to avoid striking the head or causing pain to the patient."
"If the bone is strong and hard, then you should first make a hole around it before inserting the instruments with their shallow blades."
"These are not called 'non-depth' blades but rather because they do not reach the end of the bone head and thus cannot be submerged or pass through the thickness of the bone."
"It is advisable that you take many of these blades with you, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that in all cases where the bone head is present, its sharp end should be equal in length and breadth according to the thickness of the bone."
"This is the shape of three types of trepan: The purpose of using a trepan to drill around the broken bone is that you should place the trepan on the bone and turn it with your hands until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then move the trepan to another location, keeping an interval between each stroke equal to the thickness of the ray or thereabouts."
"Then incise with instruments between each stroke of the bone, making it as subtle as possible at the end, as we have said. Do this until you remove the bone, either with your hand or for some other cause using tools such as tweezers and fine forceps which you will have prepared for that purpose."
"It is necessary that you take great care that the incision does not touch any cause of the brain."
"Then when you have completed the perforation of the bone and separated it from the brain if it was attached to it, you should extract it. It is necessary that you rasp and scrape it with an instrument similar to a scalpel but finer and sharper than the others."
"If any small bone fragments remain and are scattered about, then remove them easily with whatever instrument is appropriate. Then dress the wound with linens and dressings as we have mentioned."
"The other method of operation is easy and far from fearsome, which Gaudentius praised highly for his great skill; this is the speech about it: In the beginning, you must begin by uncovering the bone fragment in the place where the fracture is most violent and manifest, until when you have uncovered that fragment and placed beside it the pointed end of this gentle and cheerful scalpel, which has this shape:"
"And the lenticular part is for guiding and not for cutting any tissue, while the pointed end is on both sides in length, as you see. The lenticular part should be adjusted to the brain with the sharp face incident upon the bone."
"Then strike the scalpel with a small hammer so that you cut all of the bone easily as it rotates. Be sure that no panic arises in any manner, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant, certainly he would be asleep."
"Then if any attached tissue remains on the bone of the panicle in some places, cut that with the lenticular end of the scalpel and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm or fear."
If bone fracture does not penetrate into the soft tissue, and that which is cut off or removed from the bone is its outer surface, leaving behind roughness and subtle spurs, then it becomes necessary to remove this roughness with delicate razors which you have prepared in various quantities so that they may be administered at all suitable locations with the greatest precision according to how you perceive your operation on the broken bone and its shape.
You must administer your razor with which you began removing the bone using the broadest aspect of its blade and gradually proceed to use its finest edge, ensuring that you continue this process throughout until you have removed all roughness from every part of it.
Also in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer treatment according to how you perceive their rectification.
But it is necessary that they are not confused by all those who have studied this art. "And based on what we have written, we have shown this in cases of large fractures."
"And if the panniculus is retained when you excise the bone, then you should take linen according to the size of the wound, immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound; and also take another piece of linen folded or tripled, immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it on top of the first one gently so as not to press too hard on the flesh; and then apply latement, but do not stretch it except according to the size of the wound alone."
"And leave it like this for one or two days until you are sure that the wound is healing; and then remove the dressing and apply medications that promote dryness, such as lint seeds and iris flour, powdered frankincense and aloe, and similar substances."
"And if you make these medications into powders that are finely powdered and applied dry to the wound, and generally speaking, apply only those medications whose property is to cleanse without biting."
"And guard yourself according to your skill that the wound in your treatment is clean and not obscured by grease or other impurities; do not allow moisture to accumulate there, for when it settles on the membrane of the brain it corrupts it and causes harmful illness."
"And if it happens in the membrane of the head, especially when the bone has been removed and the wound is being treated, and there appears blackness on its surface; then consider whether any of the symptoms that we have mentioned occur, so that you may know that it is a serious case without doubt."
"But if the blackness appears only because of the medication that has been applied to it, and that medication is the cause of that blackness, then you should take some honey and three parts rose oil, and with those saturate a linen cloth, and gently place it on the membrane; and then treat it according to the usual methods until it heals."
"And if it happens in the membrane of the head, especially when the bone has been removed and the wound is being treated, and there appears blackness on its surface; then consider whether any of the symptoms that we have mentioned occur, so that you may know that it is a serious case without doubt."
"On restoring the nose when it is cut."
"You should know that the nose is not broken into two pieces simultaneously rising up, nor is one of those parts ever seen cut off, because it has two bones, and the cartilaginous part is not broken, and nothing happens to it except rubbing, twisting, and bending."
"But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert the little finger into its place, and gently press that fracture inward with your little finger from within and your thumb from without until the nose returns to its natural shape." "And let this be done by you easily, and take care not to cause harm to the patient through your operation."
"And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your little finger cannot reach it, then use the extremity of a reed that has been made somewhat thicker."
"And if both parts are broken, then you should make them similar to each other."
"And strive for the restoration of it on the first day after the fracture if possible; and if not, then on the seventh or tenth day you should apply cautery to the swelling."
"And insert into the extremity of the nose a wick made of linen if the fracture is in one part, or two pieces of cotton if it is in both parts, and make them thick enough to fill up the passage of the nose."
"But some of the ancient restorers say that the cotton should be wrapped in wool and changed every day, but this is not seen by me. It should rather be dipped in album of egg yolk mixed with powder of mallow; and let the wick remain there until the bone is firm and the cartilage has hardened."
"And sometimes a feather made of lion's skin is inserted into the nose in place of cotton, especially if there has been severe fracture of the nose and the patient cannot breathe through it."
"And this cause is not necessary for you. And if you wish, do that, and if you wish, use cotton."
"And if another swelling occurs after cautery, plaster the nose with wax or cerote mixed with vinegar and rose oil, or with diaclycon."
"But if there is no swelling after cautery, then you should plaster it outside with flour of sesame and powder of iris roots mixed with albumen of egg; and gently apply a lion's skin over it, and do not touch the nose for any reason."
"But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or crushed together, then you should cut over them and extract them using instruments that are appropriate for them; and then suture the incision and treat it with something that encourages healing and consolidation, and with plasters that are suitable."
"And if a ulcer appears inside the nose, then you should treat it with cotton and administer leaden tubes until it is completely healed."
"Chapter iv."
"On restoring the jawbone when it is broken."
"When the left jawbone is broken and the fracture does not have an ulcer, first examine whether the fracture is outside only, and not divided into two parts and curved inward at its ends. If it is so, then its diagnosis will be easy."
"And then, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is on the left jawbone, insert your right index finger. And push gently with that finger the swelling of the fracture inside at its ends outside, using your other hand to press the outside part of the bone in a similar way."
"And if the jawbone fracture is already separated into two parts, then you should apply traction to both parts according to a straight line until it is possible to reduce the fracture."
"And if some teeth are loose due to dislocation, then strengthen those which you expect will remain with gold or silver wire or silk thread. And then plaster the broken jawbone with cerote, and apply a folded cloth on top of it, and apply a large round piece of felt well made, or a sole of leather stretched out along the length of the jawbone. And finally bind it gently from above according to how you have prepared the bandage and what is appropriate for its application, taking care not to destroy it."
"And instruct the patient to rest quietly and avoid drinking hot liquids."
"And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in some way, then on the third day try to remove it and correct what has been altered from it. And plaster it with powder of mallow, albumen of egg, or flour of sesame after removing the cerote, and apply a lion's wool cloth over the plaster."
"And then when the plaster is attached and adjusted over it, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it there until it is healed and strengthened. For many times this fracture heals in three weeks."
"And if another time an ulcer forms after the cautery, then apply what has been said many times in this book for its healing, and remove that ulcer."
"And if the fracture is complicated by plague, then consider whether it is due to separation or suppuration. If it is due to suppuration, then be careful to remove that suppuration with whatever is appropriate for its removal among the instruments."
If the opening of the plague wound is narrow, then widen it using forceps according to your need.
And when you have finished those procedures, and nothing remains to be done, then if the opening of the plague wound is wide, but if not, place over it one of the plasters that are suitable for this purpose and cover it until it is healed.
Chapter five
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Frequently, the collarbone breaks at its midpoint in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it breaks, either it is separated, or it is separated into two parts without any displacement of the fragments; this type of fracture is easy to restore. Or else, it is such that displacement has occurred in it, and restoration of it is difficult. Or else, it is such that the fracture is accompanied by a plague wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is not accompanied by a plague wound, is that you should have two assistants present, so that one may hold theadjuster and follow the broken collarbone, while the other extends the neck on either side of the fracture.
Then carefully approximate the fragments with your fingers within such time that the desired form is achieved without any prominence or concavity at the site of the fracture.
And if more extension is needed, then place a pad of linen or wool on the side of the wound, its size according to your need. Then extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you have approximated the fracture as desired.
And if you are unable to draw the end of the collarbone out beyond its fragments, then have the patient sit up on his haunches and place a bolster made of thick cloth under him.
And the assistant presses on the muscles of either side of the fragments until the bone of the collarbone is elevated above its position in front. Then you shall reduce and approximate the fracture with your fingers as described above.
But if you feel that there are displacements of the collarbone and they are mobile, then incise over it and remove those fragments with ease.
And if the fragment is retained in the bone, then make an incision on it with one of the incissors that you have prepared for this purpose. This instrument is made of wood or iron and has the following shape:
And it resembles a spoon with this concavity. Its width should be according to your need, depending on the size of the bone or the prominence of the fragment.
But its length should be as great as possible for the operation; it should have two extremities like this, one being broad and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you made when you removed the fragment from the bone is large and you fear an abscess will form, then approximate the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small or you fear an abscess will form, then fill the wound with linen and powder according to the size of the wound.
And if an abscess forms, then take some linen dipped in sour wine and rose oil mixed with wine, and apply it over that area.
And all of this is done so that the splint does not move over the broken bone. And you should check the dressing every day to see if the bandage has softened or if the splint has shifted, and if it has, then correct it and tighten the binding.
And then place the dressing over the bone and on both sides of it under the armpit at night so that with this elevation the assistant raises up the arm on that side, thereby elevating the fracture of the collarbone above its muscles. Or else you may bind the arm to the chest with a sling.
And do not remove the dressing until about twelve days have passed without any signs of infection or abscess having occurred in that place.
And also renew the poultice if you see that its effect is diminished, and adjust it to fit the compression and restore it until it has been restored and the fracture of the collarbone has healed.
And in about twenty-eight days it becomes strong and comfortable. But sometimes in some people this takes less time than that.
Chapter VI
On the restoration of fractures of the shoulder
The blade of the scapula is broken on its lateral side, and only its margins are broken.
And so when the blade of the scapula is broken and it is only known by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare for its reduction and reduce it in every way that you can by ingenuity.
And then place powdered flour with egg white and linen on it, and also place a folded cloth poultice. Then also place over that a thin board splint according to the size of the entire blade or slightly larger.
But if on one side of the splint there is a concavity in some places on the blade, then fill that concavity with linen and also make the splint even.
And then bind it firmly above with a long bandage having downward compression and with firmness of the splint so that it does not shift from its place.
Check the dressing every day. And whenever it becomes softened, change it and tighten it again and adjust the splint if it has shifted from its place.
Keep the limb on its own side.
The blade of the scapula is restored in twenty days or at most twenty-five.
And so when this number is completed, then you should dissolve the dressing, and be sure that it is of those bones whose union is not delayed nor their healing slowed.
And if you proceed from the site of the broken bone and place your hand on the shoulder blade, then incise there and drain it if necessary, and do as we have said regarding the fracture of the collarbone for treating an abscess if one should occur here.
De restauracio de fractura de la clavicula.
"To treat a broken leg."
"The leg is sometimes fractured in the middle of it and this can happen even if the ends are only cracked or shattered."
"And when the middle of it is broken in accidents, it leans towards one side and causes pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, fever, and vomiting blood. The fracture can be felt in the cavity of the bone and is not hidden to the touch."
"And in the treatment of it, the patient should be placed on their back and a cushion put between their thighs; then press gently upon the muscles of the leg and adjust the bones of the leg as well as possible, according to all methods that are appropriate and convenient, until the shape of the bone is restored."
"And then apply bandages and gauze or linen cloths soaked in saltwater or chalk water and similar things, carefully wrapping them around the broken bone so that it is not moved. And sometimes tie a splint on the back to maintain its roundness, and tighten this with proper binding. And observe the dressing at all times, tightening it when it becomes loose."
"And if it is necessary to remove the dressing due to corruption or pressure, pain, swelling, or an abscess occurring in that place, then gently remove and replace the dressing, and correct whatever has happened there with care using proper methods. And then reapply the splint, sometimes changing its position; and adjust the bindings until healing occurs."
**Chapter VIII.**
"For the healing of the ribs when they are crushed."
"Know that fractures of the ribs do not occur except in large spaces where there is flesh on the back; and at their ends, near the cartilage, it rarely happens except for that reason." "And its recognition is not hidden from feeling through inquiry with them."
"And the healing should be done as follows: align the fracture with the said servant according to the most possible manner, so that it may be healed according to what is necessary, and properly apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel if necessary."
"And if the fracture of the ribs is declinant towards its ends within, then it often happens that there is sudden sharp pain and puncture, such as occurs in those who have suffered pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane. And it also happens with difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood; and this is difficult to cure."
"And already the Ancients knew how to treat that with many remedies."
"Thus they say: Cover the patient with warm foods that generate heat and windiness, so that the belly may be inflated and extended, and the fracture pushed outward."
"However, we should avoid this because it could cause an easy boil to form, unless otherwise necessary. And if one were to occur, this would help alleviate it."
"They said some of them that it should be placed on windiness and open so that it may suck strongly; and this seems like reasoning, but however it is feared that the windiness may draw the superfluities to the place due to weakness."
"Some of them said: Cover the place with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the wrapping may fit well according to its roundness; and let the illness be treated with pleurisy cure of food and medicine."
"And if the illness causes violent constriction which is not endured, and the bone pierces the velament with a puncturing wound, then they fear about the illness. Therefore, one should cut over the place, uncover the broken rib, and immediately place upon it that instrument which preserves the ciaphac, the memory of which has been previously mentioned, and gently cut the bone and extract it."
"And also, if the wound is large, sew its two lips with stitches, and dress it with plasters until it heals."
"And if an abscess forms inside, then take some powder and mix it with rose oil and apply it to the place, and let the illness be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess."
"And he should be propped up on pillows so that he may sit while sleeping until he is healed."
"Chapter Nine."
"Regarding the restoration of fractures of the spinal column in the back and neck."
Sometimes it happens that fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and sometimes it happens to their articulation, similarly with the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if it will heal or not, then examine whether sometimes his muscles are softened, stunned, and deadened so that they can neither move nor extend nor contract. And when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he does not feel any pain there, then you know that it has not healed. In such cases it is mortal.
But if he can move those muscles and feels constriction with pressure or puncture there, then you know that the bone is saved and the illness will go away with treatment.
But if it seems that the vertebrae of the neck are similar to this case and you want to know if they will heal or not, then consider their position.
Then if you find that some of them are already softened, it happened as in this case. And especially when he lies on his back, he feels ventilation and expulsion without his will, and sometimes he is raised up on his back and wants to urinate but cannot. Then you know that it is mortal, and at that point do not bother with its treatment.
"But if none of those occurs, the cause is better and more lightweight."
"The treatment for it is that you apply sedation to the abscess that has formed over the inflamed and infected area with some cotton soaked in hot water; place it there several times a day until the abscess is sedated."
"And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and press it onto that area with a bandage. Command the patient to rest and remain calm, and not sleep on the side where they feel pain until they are healed."
"And if the inflammation of the bone sets in or some other cause that has already separated from it occurs, then you must cut into the flesh and remove that bone. Then join the two lips of the wound together with stitches if it is large; and then treat it with a poultice made from honey, until it heals."
"But if the head of the bone is broken off and it is the end of the cure, then you must insert your left thumb into the anus and grasp the broken bone with your other hand according to what is possible by means of a traction device. Then apply a poultice or plaster and bandage it."
"And if you feel broken fragments within it, then you must open that up and remove them, and treat the wound with the appropriate cure until it is healed."
Chapter X
Of restoration of the ankle.
The bones of the ankles are sometimes broken and when they are, their fractures are only contusions at their extremities and they incline toward the inside. And it happens that the patient feels pain in that place and puncturing, and the foot becomes stiff when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you should stretch your hand over it until it is above the fracture as shown in the figure.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you should grasp it according to what appears appropriate for traction until it is brought together in its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or one that already inclines towards the inside of the foot, then you should have the patient lie on his/her stomach until you are prepared to perform the reduction of that fracture. And then when you have grasped it, apply a plaster and also apply splints of wood or bone, and bind them tightly without fear of puncturing the fracture or displacing the splints.
And then fill in the concave part with whatever is at hand so that it takes up the pressure equally and commands the patient to sleep on their back or side.
But if an inflammatory swelling occurs, then you should cease the extension and reduction of it until the inflammation has subsided, and apply whatever is appropriate to subdue that inflammation. And then return to the reduction of it and bind it as is necessary.
And if fragments or splinters occur in the bone, then you should remove them
Chapter XI
Of restoration of fracture of the clavicle
The clavicle is that which is between the chest and the head of the shoulder
And if it is broken, then its restoration is by one of two methods, the first of which is that you should take a flat piece of wood of moderate thickness in this way:
And let there be attached at both ends two cords and then it shall be suspended from an elevated place
And then have the patient sit on a chair and then place their broken arm over the wood so that it reaches to its tip in the middle part where the curvature of the wood is attached
And then suspend some heavy weight from it, or have the patient extend it towards the left side and then the physician should hold both of its ends together so that the fracture returns to its proper position
The other method is that the patient should be propped up on their neck and have their collarbone suspended by a cord. And then command two assistants to hold it, one above the fracture with both hands and the other below it, and each of them should extend towards his side
And if you wish for the extension to be stronger, then bind it with one cord above the fracture and another below it, and each of the assistants should extend towards their respective sides
Therefore if the fracture is near the extremity of the muscle, then half of the cord should be placed on the tip and the other half below the fracture towards the chest
Similarly if the fracture is close to the chest, then the cord should be placed on that location and also on the chest
Grasp it gently and without struggle and then when it has been grasped as is necessary and applied properly, then bind it if inflammation does not occur
But if inflammation occurs, then leave the splint on for seven days and apply to it a compress of flaxseed boiled in vinegar and rose oil until the inflammation has subsided, and then bind it
And the method of binding it is that you should place plaster on the fracture and then place a wrapping of moist cloth over the plaster and then adjust the arm over that clavicle and place its covered hand on its own muscle
And then wrap the cloth around it and bind the arm over the clavicle so that the arm is in its proper position, unless some cause prevents it from doing so due to the broken bone
And then apply splints.
And then place on the fracture that splint which is broader and stronger than the others and insert between each splint the width of a finger and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on either side
And then bind over the splints with the splint which was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter
And ensure that your binding over the fracture is as tight as possible And when the fracture has healed, then the binding should be looser
Therefore if you find yourself in the situation of having applied splints and binding as we have said at the time when you are restoring the limb to its proper position, then do so
And if you fear inflammation, then leave the binding and splints on for seven days as we have said
And then consider the cord in all parts for three days so that it does not cause pressure or inflammation there, or so that food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess tightness of the binding, and therefore correct everything as we have said
But if you suspect that nothing will happen due to your carelessness, then do not loosen the bandage but rather wait for three days or more
And let the patient sleep on their back with their hands on their stomach.
And place a bolster full of wool on the clavicle and ensure at all times during the night or day that neither the figure of the broken limb is disturbed nor that the cord becomes softened, so that you may correct everything with your skill
And provide food for the patient according to the order which we have mentioned earlier, so that the food is soft at first until binding begins and then gradually make it thicker.
As for the custom of the splints and bandages, they should be bound for forty days. Then they should be loosened and a bath given and medicinal ointments applied which can correct them
And if the fracture has been properly healed, then do not remove the binding and splints until after ten days or two months
"Chapter Twelve."
"On the restoration of a broken arm."
"The arm is composed of two bones called the two forks; one of which is small and is that which holds the aforementioned fist, while the other is large and is placed next to it on the side facing downwards."
"And sometimes only the larger fork bone breaks, or only the smaller one; but rarely do they break together."
"When the little fork bone breaks at the top, its restoration and healing will be easier."
"But when it breaks below the fracture, its restoration is difficult and its healing takes a long time."
"And even worse than that is when two bones break together."
"Therefore if the bone that is broken is the small fork above, then it is advisable for the doctor to perform a simple reduction with ease before it heals."
"But if the large fork is what has broken, then traction must be applied forcefully."
"And if both forks are broken together, then you should apply traction even harder."
"And you should place the figure of the hand in its proper position during reduction and traction by extending it on a pillow; placing the thumb above all other fingers, the little finger lower than the others, and the patient lying on their side facing upwards."
"And place a pillow in front of them at the level or behind the elbow to prevent him from moving around."
"Now have one assistant extend the elbow below with their own hand or with linen, and another assistant extend it above in the same manner."
"And now let the surgeon reduce the bone into place by aligning it according to its best possible position."
"If there are any irregularities in the fracture of the bone, then you should press all your weight on it to reduce it back into place."
"But if there is separation of the bone ends with displacement of the elbow joint that does not reduce spontaneously, then you should proceed as follows:..."
"But if the fracture is compounded (open), I have already discussed this case separately. Therefore, take care of that."
"And if you encounter an aposteme at the beginning of your reduction, then take a cloth smeared with cerot made from rose oil and white wax, which should be neither too thick nor too thin. Place this cloth over the aposteme with subtle tension until it subsides. Then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared with powdered mallow mixed with egg whites, and also apply plaster strips; make sure that the plaster strip placed on the fracture is wide and strong."
"And know that in the case of fractures of the arm, there are usually six plaster strips applied, either for a fracture of one fork or for two forks together."
"Now apply your traction even harder and more vehemently over the fracture site, and if necessary, increase the tension even further on the proximal or distal parts of the fracture according to what is described at the beginning of this chapter."
"And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moistened with water, not hard or rough. The bandage used for tension should be of moderate thickness between subtle and thick, as recommended earlier."
"Keep the limb immobilized for several days." "And if there is any cause that requires repositioning, such as swelling of the limb, then you should immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling subsides."
"Leave the limb at rest without traction for a few days and then reapply the traction."
"And if the traction has been sufficiently maintained and the bone has already reduced well, then you should now finish everything with your own strength."
"And if there is any obstruction of nourishment to the limb due to excessive traction, then you should relax it a little and leave it alone for a few days until circulation is restored, and then apply traction again."
"But if the limb shows no signs of any of the conditions mentioned earlier, then there is no need to remove the traction for more than about 20 days or so."
"Then have the patient use his own strength to move his arm around and make him lie on his back to sleep."
"And know that this fracture is usually restored in 30 to 32 days, but sometimes it can be restored in as few as 28 days, depending on the condition of the complexion and the strength of the patient."
"Chapter XIII."
"On the reduction of fractures of the thumb and fingers."
"Know that at the tip of the hand and in the joints of the fingers there are often fractures, not actual breaks but frequent dislocations."
"And when there is fracture or dislocation of the hand, the patient should be made to lie on their back with their arms extended out in front of them, and place their other hand over that one to keep it steady."
"Then the servant should extend the broken bones and the physician will align them, making sure they fit together well with proper alignment."
"And then a plaster and bandage should be applied, unless there is already a wound that needs cauterizing. Afterwards, apply a splint according to the size of the location, and you have already wrapped it in linen."
"But if the fracture is on the palm side of the hand, then make a sling from cloth and tell the patient to hold it with their palm pressed against theirs. Afterwards, bind it securely with a long piece of cloth."
"And the splint should be made of leather wrapped in cloth that is elevated on one side so that the leather touches the palm. Then bind it securely as needed."
"But if the fracture is on the outer side of the hand, then apply a splint on top and another splint from the edge of the palm to the wrist so that the hand is upright and straight. Then apply the bandage in this way, as if the hand were turned towards the little finger between those parts."
"And if there is a fracture in one of the knuckles of those parts, then if it's the thumb, align it as needed and bind it to the palm. And if you wish, apply a small splint on the outside to elevate the fracture and prevent movement."
"But if the fracture is in one of the other knuckles, such as at the middle joint or the index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then align and bind it with the finger on which it occurs. Or they can all be bound together continuously, which is better; or apply a small splint over that area, as we have said for the thumb."
"And when you are performing your restoration and bandaging, consider the location of the wound, cauterization, and opposition according to what is needed in case any cause occurs that has been mentioned previously."
"Chapter XIV."
"On the restoration of fractures of the bone of the ankle."
"The bone of the ankle is often broken and declares itself by becoming displaced at the joint in front and being pushed upwards."
"And the restoration is done in such a way that a bandage is applied above the fracture and another below it. The patient should be lying on their stomach with their face down."
"And the minister should extend all the bandages to his party according to the quality when the fracture is in the middle of the bone."
"If the fracture is at the root of the thigh bone, then draw the thread tightly to its edge near the knee joint, so that the tension comes out from there, and let another thread remain on the break."
"If the fracture is at the knee joint, then draw the thread tightly to the knee, and let the tension come out from there."
"And then the doctor presses both bones together at the two ends so that they reduce it back to its natural shape and the bone is joined together by good union. Then you must apply a plaster and strapping if there is no inflammation of the member." "But if there is an abscess, then leave it alone for several days until the abscess has softened, and then return to your treatment."
"The strapping should be done like this: you must wrap a hard bandage over the fracture two or three times and remove any excess; then wrap the limb so that it reaches to the ankle and root of the thigh."
"And insert a long needle between the thigh and the bandage near the knee joint, bringing the ends of the thread from above on both sides, then wrap it around the limb."
"And then press a pad over the thigh at that fracture point, and attach a splint to the outer surface of the bandage."
"And then fill in the spaces between the limb and the thigh with soft cloths so that the strapping remains firm."
"And then begin with a tight strapping of the middle over the fracture point using three or four wraps, and as you move away from the fracture point, make your strapping looser and softer."
"And then extend both ends of the thread to the sides until they reach the heel of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splint on the other side so that the limb does not move from its position."
"And then leave the strapping on for several days without causing any injury to the limb or abscesses or inflammation or swelling resembling those described earlier. If anything happens, remove the strapping immediately and correct it as soon as possible, according to what we have said many times."
"But if the bone sticks out from its proper place, you must reduce it back into position as much as possible. If this cannot be done, then apply a splint and remove it once healing has occurred."
"And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with splints other than those that have been mentioned in the treatise and in the arm."
"But nevertheless, in restoring the limb in this manner there should be no limpiness caused by shortening. And if it is restored alone, without the other leg being applied, then necessarily that limb will remain lame."
"And know that the thigh is strengthened in forty days or a little more or less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions."
"Chapter Fifteen"
"On the reduction of fractures of the collarbone."
"You should know that fracture of the kneecap can occur suddenly, but it often happens due to direct impact."
"And if there is fracture, it is only a split or crack in its parts, and this is an illness or small injury, and overall it has been felt as such."
