"And the repair of it is easy, so that what has been broken in its parts should be united again with these sutures that have been added and joined together as much as possible for equanimity and ease of formation."
"And therefore pause with plaster and pausing over that round surface, if you need to do so; and stretch over it with this binding accordingly."
"And also consider all the dispositions that we have recommended for other fractures, thus like cauterization and similar applications as much as possible so that it pauses on all accidents with whatever is convenient for its healing."
"Chapter XVI"
"On the repair of fracture of the knee-cap."
"You should know that in the kneecap there are two bones, of which one is thick and is called by the name of the kneecap; and the other is thin, and is called patella."
"And it happens with some species of fracture that it occurs to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its repair is made in the same way as the repair of the arm is made, and the operation for it is one."
"And if both bones are broken at once, the kneecap becomes completely fragmented."
"If it is broken, the bone becomes twisted at its ends."
"And if it is crushed but not displaced, then those are cases which are not serious."
"And especially apply extension, traction and splinting of the bones according to that method."
"But consider this, if the fracture of the thigh bone is not accompanied by many complications, then the extension should be lighter and gentler, according to your strength."
"And when operating on the thigh bone, after you have straightened the bones and completed your operation, then you will take pieces of wood from a tree, which is placed near those causes that are placed in usual positions, and these are made equally from branches of palm; choose those that are light, not too heavy or large, and their length should be equal to the length of the thigh bone, from the knee to the hip."
"And then wrap each piece of wood in two layers according to its length, and place one on the upper thigh at one end and the other on the other end, and they should be placed from ten inches below the hip joint downwards."
"And then tie the two splints in three places at both extremities and at the middle point, because with this structure the thigh bone is prevented from bending to the right or left side, and it stands for good position."
"And now you will administer a splint of wood according to the length of the thigh bone, and place it on it so that it may preserve its position."
"And especially this should be done in fracture cases, and when such an injury occurs properly."
"And then examine the thigh bone every day and study it carefully to see if there is any inflammation or swelling or other similar causes. And whenever such cause occurs, you should apply that which is necessary according to the condition for its healing."
"Chapter XVII."
Of the fracture of the ankle bone and bones of the foot.
Not the ankle bone alone suffers fracture all the time; but some bones of the foot suffer fracture occasionally; and those of the foot seldom suffer fracture, and do not suffer it unless they are out of their usual position.
And if the bones of the foot suffer fracture and those bones are sometimes elevated above one another among themselves, then the patient places his foot on the ground prepared thus, as going.
And near upon this he places one foot upon that which is elevated by those bones; and near upon it presses them down and squeezes them round about in the places.
And near upon this he applies plaster and stops up above; and places on the front of the foot a small table with two flat heads.
And near upon this he stretches that limb downwards with the foot; because thou hast enveloped it with cloths and other things of which thou standest in need.
And when three or four days have passed thou shalt then unbind it; for thou wilt find the bones squeezed together, either broken or disjointed; and the operation on these is as follows.
But if some of them are broken, then restore these; and squeeze them together thus with glue and sew as thou hast been taught in the restoration of those of the hand. And near upon this place the splint along the length of the toe and let it be thus made somewhat wider at the end; and near upon this place on the foot that table which hath been recommended to thee, and stretch that with a downward strain.
But if that which is broken of the bones are two or three or more, then place over each toe a splint according to its quantity wrapped in lion's skin; and stretch the table on the lower part of the foot and let it stand upon one of the flat heads at the front of the foot so that it may have good retention.
And it is necessary for thee that thou dost not forget to consider all those causes which are mentioned to thee in the other fractures of accidents that have been spoken of; and apply thyself to each accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter XVIII
Of the fracture of the thigh bone of a woman, of the bone of the hip joint and of the penis.
When the thigh bone of a woman is broken, then make that cease in this manner, and near upon this incline it somewhat at the joint of the two legs behind.
And near upon this the midwife shall bind it with soft cloth and let it be made firm in the thigh thus as an egg; and near upon this let the woman lie on her back and elevate little by little one buttock; for all this is done until that is before the joint of the thigh thus as an egg, and the broken bone returns.
And near upon this apply poultices to the buttock; and when she wishes to urinate remove the bandage with ease and let her urinate; and reduce it again and return it according to the manner in which thou hast first placed it on; and then return to the first poultice.
And do thus for seven days or thereabouts, for that is restored.
And if thou wilt, take once a sheep's bladder and stretch it over the breach of that one with a reed cane; and put all the bladder into the thigh bone and then blow into the reed with breath until the bladder be inflated within the thigh bone, for the fracture returns.
And near upon this let it be bound up with soft cloth and rest thus for some days according to that which hath been recommended until it be healed.
And when the bone of the hip joint of a man or woman is broken, then administer to the restoration and reduction of it that which hath been recommended in the bone of the hip.
And that which is right is not concealed in these fractures near to the body and easily done, especially to him to whom this my book is some study and use; for that fracture signifieth to thee in the greater dispositions a straight way rightly in the restoration and the strapping of it, and therefore thou shalt understand well.
But in the penis of a man when it is broken, take a goose feather and put the penis into that; and near upon this apply over it wrapping of cloth and let it be wrapped up, and then let it lie for three days or thereabouts, and it shall be healed.
Chapter XIX
"For the healing of bone fractures when they are inflamed."
"It is convenient for that person who has suffered a fracture with inflammation, and especially if the bone is large like those of the thigh or arm, or similar. Therefore, you should immediately flebotomize that person if conditions for bloodletting are suitable as mentioned above."
"If the inflammation flows with blood, then you should cover it immediately and powder it with this mixture:..."
"And thus begins to restore the fracture on that same day, without delay, unless there is an abscess."
"But if an abscess occurs, you should leave the restoration for 9 days until it has healed. And do not approach it on the third day or fourth day altogether, because bad accidents may occur."
"But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin and exposed, you should carefully reduce it and align it with your hands using gentle force without excessive tension."
"But if the reduction is not well prepared and equaling it with your hands alone causes excessive tension, then you should use this instrument to reduce it: it is made of iron, seven or eight digits long and its width according to the amount of inflammation. And so it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these instruments ready at hand for all kinds of fractures."
"And once it is reduced and properly aligned, apply a small amount of pressure so that it is not doubled during the operation."
"And if there are sharp edges at the ends of the bone fragments, you should carefully remove them with the forceps and reduce the size of the fragments as much as possible."
"And this is how it looks:"
"It is called in Greek 'labre volentz atelarem', meaning it is a rare case."
"For what reason must the extremity of his foot be elevated above the prominence of the bone?"
"And press him in a way that makes the bone return to its original position, and keep it there with some pressure."
"And then try to align the extremities of the fracture between them. If the extremity of the broken bone is thin and does not fit well with the instrument, you should insert the extremity of that bone into the tool so that it has enough power."
"But if you cannot reduce the bone with what has been recommended, then cut it using one of the instruments mentioned earlier, or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you."
"And also rasp that which remains on the bone from sharp edges and small chips."
"And when the bone has been reduced and during the reduction the patient feels intense pain on moving, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position. Therefore do this if you want to greatly benefit the patient."
"When you have completed your treatment of the bone, then immerse it in black soap and water, especially if it is summer, and do not pour any grease or oil over it for fear that putrefaction and corruption may occur."
"And also apply splints at the time of completion of the bone treatment and leave the wound exposed until you insert them with forceps and use bandages according to the quantity of the wound."
"And be very careful that you do not strain the wound with the fracture, for many times fools have done this and caused harm to their patients with death or rotting flesh or other mischief. This is unhealthy." "And if your traction is light, soft, different from the usual traction used for fractures."
"And if the wound is large or a bad wound and you fear that one of those unfortunate accidents may occur which have been mentioned earlier, and there is pain in the area, then it is not necessary to apply splints. Instead, make a dressing with hard cloths instead of splints and press it on."
"And when the wound begins to form scab about one or two days after applying the dressing, then remove it and apply poultices, which are used according to our custom for healing wounds, such as 'tetrafarmacum' and similar ones."
"And it is fitting for you to cleanse the wound and examine it every day morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and it is necessary that the member be prepared in such a way that putrefaction flows easily from it towards its openings."
"If many days have passed and the wound is not consolidated nor separated from the scab, then you should know that it is because of the small vessels. Therefore, explore the wound with a probe." "And those vessels that are separated and removed or extracted should be left alone. Those that cannot be separated and cause pain in the member should be pursued and incised according to what you consider possible."
"If pus or ichor occurs in the wound, it is called 'stymia', or some other kind of corruption and putrefaction. Then you should apply one of those with which you consider appropriate for its cure, according to what has been described earlier in its chapter."
"And about this, in which it is fitting that I speak briefly and you weigh your patience: when the bone is crushed, do not presume to extract it or remove it because many times death has occurred for this reason. Leave it there until putrefaction occurs, for after twenty or thirty days it will fall out on its own. Then treat the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it."
"Chapter XX."
"Of the treatment of swelling that occurs at the site of any fracture."
"Very often this swelling occurs at the site of fracture healing, especially near joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb and hinders its natural operation."
"Therefore, if the swelling is recent, apply to it medicines that have astringent properties such as aloe, resin, myrrh, and similar ones. Take some or all of them and mix them with wine that has been thickened, or with albumen of egg, or with vinegar, and apply this mixture to the swelling on linen cloth, binding it tightly in place."
"And leave the dressing on for several days without removing it. Then remove it and apply another one until the swelling has disappeared. Or press a thin sheet of heated lead onto it, because lead has the property of reducing all swellings in the limbs."
"But if the swelling has become hard and stiff and requires removal with caution, then incise it along its dominant seam and remove the excess or excise it with a rasp until it is removed. Then treat it until it is healed."
"Chapter XXI."
"Of the restoration of fracture when it has healed but the limb remains thin and weak, not following its natural course."
"When a fracture of the bone has healed, but the limb remains thin and weak, this occurs for many reasons: either because of too much solution of the liga
"The treatment for this is to give the patient good food to make his blood thicker, so that the blood is multiplied in him. And apply baths, and massage and laughter upon it."
"And also apply plaster onto the limb so that the plaster attracts much nourishment to it, or continue massaging it with warm water until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural shape."
"Chapter XXII."
"When broken bones are restored crookedly, forbid their operation."
"When a limb previously restored becomes crooked or prominent during the healing process due to a break and swelling of the bone, it may appear deformed but nonetheless the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation; therefore do not accept the opinion of anyone who considers that limb newly broken."
"However, many foolish doctors and restorers have done this in our land; and this operation is greatly to be condemned and causes great fear, the sign of which is deadly."
"However, if lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be fomented with hot water in which softening herbs have been cooked, such as marshmallow root and rose hips, mallows, and similar ones; and a poultice should be made with a mollifying plaster like dallaquilon well-made; or the juice of marshmallow root should be pressed out and mixed with groats of wheat or oil of cinnamon and applied as a poultice; or fresh figs should be pressed and trampled with pigeon dung, and similar to those medicines called amulets for consolidation."
"And sometimes it is dissolved with frequent gentle friction which should be done in the most humid parts, and movement of the member should be administered at all times."
"However, if the deformity is already established and strong and turned into bone, and there is a need for treatment with an iron instrument, then it is necessary to cut the excess bone above and dissolve the continuity of the bone, and remove the superfluous swelling of that member with subtle incissors, and facilitate movement in that limb with diligence and care."
"Chapter XXII."
"Of speech in dislocation."
Dislocation is the displacement of some of the joints in its place, so that it is prevented from moving, and the figure of the member is disfigured, causing great pain and injuries to the patient.
Therefore when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to promptly reduce it so that it may be reduced quickly, because if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and very difficult to reduce.
Moreover, during this inflammation period, it should not be moved or stretched because many times it can cause convulsions and intense pain.
However, if this happens, then it is necessary that you have recourse to the cauterization of the patient and let him rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the member should be wrapped in hot water and oil, and thus reduced with ease.
And all members should be treated with what will bring them back to their original position.
And I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation as it was previously explained in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to the lower parts.
Chapter XXIV
Of the treatment of dislocation of the jawbone.
So if both jaws are dislocated and not reduced, the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they are displaced from their places by slight movement and become softened slightly; or they are completely dislocated, allowing saliva to flow freely until they are softened at the joint before they can be returned to their places nor close the mouth, and the tongue is wrapped in his handkerchief.
But if the dislocation is slight, then often it returns to its place by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and complicated, then reduction must be administered gently, without delay at all.
And let the assistant hold the patient's head, and insert his fingers of one hand into the depression of the dislocation of the jawbone within the mouth of the patient if the dislocation is on one side; or let him insert both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and let the other fingers be outside the cheeks to balance them; and command the patient to close his jaws and make them still in each part; and the doctor balances the jawbone and presses it until it returns to its proper place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated at once, then evaporation with hot water and oil should be administered, until easy reduction of both can be made without delaying reduction at all, as we have said.
And then when they have returned and are balanced, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then apply poultices of cloth with cerote, and let it be made of wax and olive oil; and let it be bound with facility with soft linen.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head be placed between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left; and do not force him to open his mouth for any cause until after the pains are alleviated and the jaws are healed. Then let him eat soft food, until when he wishes to chew something.
And let this be administered with ease, and do not labor over the opening of the mouth during eating and drinking and speaking until the jawbone is healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and do not return to their places, then often it happens that fevers and continuous sweats occur, and strong laxatives are given to the patient's belly, and strong purgatives made of hellebore.
And when you do this, then know that it is harmful; for many times the patient dies in ten days to whom this happens.
Chapter XXV
Of the reduction of dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The shoulder blade is not displaced within due to its continuity with the chest; sometimes it may be displaced at the outset, which is evident by touch.
To restore it, have the patient lie on their back and extend their arms. Then press the spot with your palm firmly, for it will return to its place.
Then apply a plaster and dusting powder, and bind that area tightly.
However, if the end of that which attaches to it is not displaced on any day, then it should be reduced and immobilized according to what we have said and according to your skill.
Then apply a plaster, dusting powder, and bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to calmness and rest until they are healed.
And with this same treatment, the end of the muscle is reduced when it becomes separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI
Of the reduction of muscle dislocation
You should know that muscles are not displaced except in three ways, one of which is when they are displaced from their attachment to the tendinous insertion.
And the second way is when they are displaced from their midbelly, which happens rarely; and they are not displaced at attachments behind by the shoulder blade or in front by nerves.
Muscles are most frequently displaced and exited at their tendinous insertions near the tibiae, especially in those individuals with less flesh where displacement occurs more easily. However, in those with abundant flesh, muscles exit with difficulty.
Sometimes it happens to certain men that through a blow or fall, the muscle becomes bruised and swollen with blood, causing the muscle to become displaced.
Therefore, you should immediately address this condition while it is still recent, and then proceed with its treatment.
Muscle displacement is recognized when it occurs at its tendinous insertion near the tibia. Thus, you can distinguish between a displaced muscle and an intact one by observing the obvious differences between them. You will find concavity at the head of the muscle with the tibia near the touch of the head of the muscle like a horse's hoof. The patient cannot bring their hand to their ear nor move that limb in any way.
If it is displaced from the foot or its middle part, you will immediately find a prominent touch and not hidden.
From this displacement, easy reduction can be made when it is recent and the patient is a child.
"The reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand above the parts; and then places their thumbs on either side of the patient's titillic; and raises the joint with strength to its proper place, and the minister raises the patient's hand, and extends that one upwards; and then keeps that from returning lightly."
"If this does not return as described above, and if the dislocation first occurred many days ago, then it is necessary for the patient to be bathed in hot water; and they should also be given an ointment that softens and lubricates like this: take equal parts of rosehips, fenugreek, and poppy seeds in water, mix well, and administer."
"And then the patient should be propped up on their back and kept still around the titillic area until the joint is between levitation and structure; and then the doctor places their heel on the sphere, lifts the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's arm so that it does not move from its place; another assistant should also hold the patient's head to prevent movement."
"And if you wish to reduce it in this manner: take a man who is taller than the patient and place him on the same side of the patient. Then insert his elbow into the titillic area of the patient's arm and elevate the titillic upwards while keeping the patient suspended in air; another assistant should pull the patient's arm downwards towards their own belly." "But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that another cause be suspended along with them, otherwise they will fall and the dislocation will return quickly."
"And it can also be reduced in this manner: place on the ground a long stake with a rounded head like a pestle, neither too thick nor too thin, and place the patient leaning against it. Then cover the top of the stake with soft cloths, have the patient stand along the length of the stake with their arm extended towards the other side; and extend their body towards the other side with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly."
"And if reduction is difficult due to all the causes mentioned above, then use this cure: take a stake that is two braccia in length, four fingers in width, and whose thickness should be reduced at the titillic according to this shape:"
"And then place soft cloths on the rounded head of the stake to prevent injury to the patient."
"And then place the stake against the titillic of the patient, extend their arm or forearm along the stake towards the other side, and tie the stake onto the assistants' arms and hand using cloths."
"And then place the arm on a step of stairs according to its width, extend the hand towards the other side, and suspend the rest of the body on the other side, for the joint will enter quickly."
"And once the joint has entered according to each method that is possible for you, then place a pad of wool felt over the titillic. Then apply a plaster made from powdered brick dust mixed with eggs and albumen of egg over the entire muscle as it is wrapped upwards. Next, bind the titillic tightly with a suitable bandage; wind the lint around the plaster, fasten the patient's hand to their neck, and leave it in place for seven days without removing the hand."
"And care should be taken that the patient's diet is light during this time to facilitate recovery, for it will be easier to heal."
"And then gradually increase the patient's diet after seven or eight days and allow them some exercise. And once it has strengthened and is no longer tender, then it can be considered healed."
"But if the joint has been dislocated many times due to humidity that occurs there or for other reasons, then it is necessary to administer cautery with three trails according to what was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery."
"But if even after all that is done the joint does not heal within seven days and remains loose even when the bandage and plaster are reapplied several times, and the patient cannot raise their arm above their head, then know that the nerves at the head of the muscle have either been severed, stretched or softened. And know that the joint will never return to its proper place."
"For the dislocation of the shoulder joint that occurs near the pit and breast or behind the shoulders, reduction is performed with force and extension using the hands until it returns."
"And then apply another bandage and treat it until it heals."
"And if heaviness or slowness occurs in the limb during recovery, then have the patient take many baths until that heaviness is relieved and the limb returns to its original condition."
"Chapter XXVII."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the hip."
"Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns to its place with difficulty. And it can be dislocated in any direction, but most commonly towards the front or back. You will know if the hip has been dislocated because there will be a difference in length when standing or lying down according to how it was dislocated. When you reduce the dislocated hip with the sacrum, this will be clearly apparent to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the thigh, nor touch the muscle itself."
"And therefore, take care to reduce the dislocation promptly before an abscess forms because if an abscess does form it will be difficult to reduce and the limb may not heal completely; particularly when it is displaced backwards as this is the worst type of dislocation in terms of pain and often leads to death."
"And the reduction of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is performed by extending the patient's hand with two hands and expanding their arm. The physician places their hands on the hip and behind it, and pushes the joint back into place using their elbows or the edge of his palm until it returns to its proper position."
"But if the dislocation is towards the front, then reduce it by bending the hand with your own hand until you strike the hip bone with the edge of your palm."
"And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then apply strong extension and considerable force."
"And it is necessary for two assistants to extend the limb and hold it in that position so that it does not move backwards during extension."
"And then wrap the entire limb from front to back with a long folded cloth or bandage."
"And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should grease it with some oil to facilitate lubrication of the joint. Then push the joint forcefully back into place until it returns."
"And then apply a plaster with styptic and expectorant properties made from egg albumen over the reduced joint. Bind it tightly with a descending bandage and hang the arm in a sling for several days."
"And then after some time has passed, if the joint has healed properly in its place, then you may remove the bandage and leave it alone."
"But if you find that the joint is not strong enough, then repeat the plaster and bandage application, leaving it on for several more days until it has healed properly. Then remove it."
If it happens that the patient feels heavy during the reduction of the joint, and their condition is in movement, then administer humidification with baths and gentle friction, along with compression until they feel relieved. Or place a warm compress on the joint if it's a matter of fatty tissue; then bind it up, leaving that on the bone day and night. Then remove the compress and place the patient in a bath; once they're out, gently rub the joint with warm friction, and repeat the application of warmth if necessary, up to three times. During this process, continue bathing until relieved.
And if you wish, place a wet and warm cowhide fold on the joint edge, and press it onto them; do this several times until that becomes moistened and reduced in its condition.
Chapter XXVIII
**On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Shoulder Joint**
"Sometimes the hand is dislocated; its relocation is easy, unlike other joints, but it is necessary to act quickly in relocating it at the time of dislocation before the place becomes inflamed or an inflammation occurs."
"The relocation of the hand's dislocation involves placing the patient's hand on a table and extending it by the servant. The doctor then places their hand or palm over the prominence of the joint and pushes until it returns to its proper position."
"But if the palm is already facing towards the stomach of the hand, then during extension and reduction, the patient should place the back of their hand on a table; and if the dislocation is previous to the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand should be intrinsecely upon the table, so that the doctor's hand can cover that prominence of the joint."
"And therefore if it returns quickly, good. But if not, then press the hand with strength until the inflammation subsides and leave him; and do not insist upon this, for if not, neither suffering nor power can be done over the reduction of it after several days have passed."
"But however the joint returns according to its tortuosity; and do not notify the patient of any cause, but say that the hand is mollified and cannot strengthen itself. Then you should know that the nerves are crushed or torn, and then it is not necessary except that it be strengthened with cautery near the site of it, and afterwards confer some cause to him."
"So when the dislocation has returned, then place upon it that pressure which we have recommended, and let it be left and strengthened for 7 days. And also near to it be untied and let it rest, and the hand of it be exercised."
"But if the motion of the joint is stayed and any gravity occurs in it, then apply to it cold water with friction several times until it is alleviated."
"Chapter XXIX."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of these."
Sometimes these are dislocated from all parts.
And when some of these fingers are dislocated towards the palm or belly of the hand, then stretch the finger and reduce the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
And hold the head of the finger firm in place, suspending it from the side towards which it has been displaced, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straight within its day
And when it is night, bind it according to this narrative, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise with movement, and bind it at night. And do this for several days until it is straight
Do likewise if it is dislocated towards the belly of the hand, and bind it at that joint and perform your first operation with this until it is healed.
Do similarly in this way when it is dislocated at other joints
Chapter .xxx.
On the treatment of dislocation of the spine.
When there occurs dislocation of any of the spine or neck vertebrae and it is a complete dislocation where some of the vertebrae have gone out of their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death usually follows.
And the sign of this is that the evacuation of the bowels occurs involuntarily without being able to retain it. And often some members become paralyzed such as one leg or both legs, or both arms, or one of them; and sometimes a vertebra has gone out of its place, for many times they have gone out and many times they have been moved back into their places in four directions.
Therefore it is already dislocated to the posterior parts and is called sciatica.
But the treatment of it is that if sciatica occurs in childhood, then there is no cure for it nor is there any hope of complete recovery.
But in cases which occur due to falling or trauma or something similar to that, the Ancients have already described various methods of treatment at length, from which one can derive great benefit; but I will abbreviate some of those because of my proximity to the intention of my exposition, with which I have briefly explained and formed this book using diverse instruments with which I will show and explain it.
And only those are cured whose sciatica occurs properly in the back according to what I have mentioned.
And it is that the patient should lie on their face near a wall and stretch out their legs while being careful not to injure the thigh.
And then a straight stick should be placed upright, fixed in the ground at the end of the foot near the head of the patient, and another stick should be placed under their heels at the other end.
And one minister should hold the first stick firmly without letting it slip into a hollow, and another minister should hold the second according to that form.
And then wrap a cloth around the head of the patient and tie it firmly. And extend one end of the bandage to the stick which is above their head, and tie it there. And then stretch another bandage across their hips on both sides of them, and down towards their legs. And then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other stick that is near their feet.
And then each minister should extend the bandage to the point of the stick and ensure that neither of the two sticks nor the bandages are dislocated from their places where they were fixed, but nevertheless not tied as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the vertebrae with firmness until it returns to its place, or he can place one table there and adjust his feet on top of the table until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a plank of which the width or length is about three cubits, and cut out a space in the wall near where the patient lies into which the end of the plank can be inserted. And then place the middle of the plank on top of the vertebrae, and the physician should place his feet on the other end, and press down firmly until the vertebra is reduced and returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, then turn the vertebra using your hand; and it is that you should lie on the ground near the head of the patient with two sticks behind them, each stick being one cubit long, and place another two sticks behind the head of the patient in the same manner. And let there be a space of one palm between each stick, and already made in each stick is a hole through which the cord should pass, and let each stick be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not dislodged.
And let there also be a round stick inserted into the hole through which the cord should pass, and let this stick be turned in the two holes of the two sticks.
And at one end of it there is a hole in which the stick is formed, with its length being one palm and that which is turned. And in the other two sticks there are similar ones to this.
And then tighten the bandages that you have stretched over the thigh of the patient on the stick which is above their head, and the bandages that you have stretched over their hips on the stick which is near their feet.
And then let there be a minister at each hole who will turn the stick with his hand, and the physician should press down on the vertebra as we have said.
"And now that the spine has returned, and the area is covered, it is necessary to place a hot poultice with egg whites and lint; then place a tablecloth of suitable size over the poultice, which should be three fingers wide or approximately that size, and its length should be equal to the width of the swelling; and place your hand on the spine."
"And now it is necessary to bandage it with the material we have mentioned, until the sick person's appetite returns and they are healed."
"And if there is still some swelling at the end of healing, it is necessary to apply moistening and cleansing medicines with application through a long-term plaster, as recommended earlier; and now we will apply lead foil on that area."
"And sometimes swelling occurs at the end of the spine of the back due to dislocation of that bone; and this is the bone which already threatens death, so it should not be treated with this medicine, for strong lead will cause its sudden death."
"Chapter XXXI."
"On the treatment of a dislocated hip."
You know that the joint of the ankle and the joint of the muscle do not involve only dislocation, but also occur in those where there is little movement and concavity.
The ankle joint can be dislocated in four ways: it can dislocate inwardly, outwardly, anteriorly, or posteriorly.
It is more commonly dislocated inward and less commonly dislocated anteriorly or posteriorly.
"Symptom of dislocation in the inner parts is this: when you press the healthy thigh against the sick one, it feels longer and its outline more prominent than that of the healthy one; the patient cannot bend their leg. You should then search for the sign of an old bruise or hematoma, because the head of the matter has reached here."
"The sign that dislocation has occurred externally is that its complications are opposite to this one."
"The sign of dislocation that has occurred anteriorly is that the thigh expands according to its shape, but it cannot bend without pain. If pressed further, it cannot go beyond this point; urine retention and bruising are present, and walking is difficult."
"The sign of dislocation that has occurred posteriorly is that the thigh does not expand and cannot bend forward as far as the knee can bend backward. The thigh is shorter than the other one, the knee is softened, and the head of the condition is located there."
"But the reduction of cases of this dislocation is as follows: first, consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient. If it is, then do not attempt to reduce it yourself, but leave it as it is. However, if it is not in that condition, then reduction should be performed according to its nature."
"But in the case of a recent dislocation that belongs to one of the four types of dislocation, you should strive to bring back the joint and extend it both internally and externally. Move the leg right and left, because with this alone the condition will return and no further treatment is needed."
"But if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong bandage and extend the thigh of the leg as far back as possible, or with its muscles, or by using a loop that is tied over the upper part of the thigh. And another assistant should extend their hand over the knee joint so that they can place their fingers on the inner side of it. Then stretch tightly with a bandage made of linen placed over the ridge of the bone, and hold one end of the bandage while another assistant holds the other."
"And let the extension be from the thigh to the knee joint or from the inner side of the thigh to the outer side at the hip joint, or from the back of the thigh to the front of the shinbone."
"And let all these extensions be done at once, while the patient is lying on their back with their hips raised off the ground and their legs hanging down."
"And this type of extension is common to all four types [of dislocation]."
"And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, that is good. But if not, then it is necessary for each type to consider its own proper treatment."
"In the proper reduction of this dislocation, when it has occurred internally, the patient should be made to lie on their side facing away from the dislocated hip. Then place bandages over the ridge of the bone in the area between the head of the bone and the inner side of the thigh. And then extend the bandage upward from the front of the hip joint to as high as the outer part of the iliac crest."
"And then another strong assistant should grasp the leg and press on the large part of the dislocated hip joint and extend it strongly outward, because this will bring it back into its proper place."
"And this is one of the easiest treatments by which this member can be reduced, compared to the other types [of dislocation]."
"But if it is dislocated on top of you and does not obey this type of reduction, then you must tie both of the patient's feet together with a strong, light linen bandage over their ankles and calves."
"And let the length of each bandage be about four spans, and let the dislocated leg be extended more than the other by about two spans."
"And then suspend the patient over the head of the bed, with their hands on their hips, and let the length from the floor be about two cubits."
"And then command a strong youth to press down on the head of the bone and another youth to pull down on the leg with great force, because this will quickly return the joint to its proper place."
"But in the proper reduction of this dislocation when it occurs externally, let the patient lie on their back as we have recommended for the case of obesity. And let the bandage be tightened over the thigh of the dislocated leg and over its foot."
"And then two poles should be placed, one under the foot of the leg and the other under the head. And another pole fitted with a padded cushion should be placed in the middle, wrapped in wet cloths so that the patient is not injured by the pole being between their legs, for fear that the extension towards the outside may be drawn too strongly."
"And then each assistant should extend his respective side and the physician with one hand shall guide the reduction according to the art [of medicine]."
"But when the dislocation is at the front of the thigh, then the leg should be extended slightly. And according to this method, let the physician place his right palm on the inner side of the dislocated thigh, and then grasp it with his other hand and extend it towards the outside as far as the shinbone."
"But when the dislocation is at the back of the thigh, then it should not be extended towards the outside, but rather elevated off the ground. And let a hard support be placed underneath, just as in the case of an ankle fracture when dislocated externally, according to what we have said about the method for such fractures; and this should be done with the patient lying on their face and the bandages extended according to our instructions."
"And pressure should be applied using a board over the spot where the joint is felt."
"So when the reduction is completed as it should be, you will know that the dislocation has been reduced because you can extend both legs of the patient, and when you release them and the patient extends their leg without pain, then you will know that the member has been properly relocated. Then apply two splints and bind them firmly with a bandage so that the ankle is not displaced at any of its parts. And keep the patient at rest for three or four days."
"And then untie the bandages and splints, and compare the length of one leg with the other."
"Then if you find those things in truth, then know that the dislocation is healed, and at that point let the patient walk."
"But if you observe any signs of inflammation, then return and splint it again, extend it according to your original traction method, and let the patient rest for three more days."
"And then remove the splints and allow the patient to gradually begin walking, little by little over several days until they are fully recovered."
Chapter XXXII
Of the treatment of dislocation of the ankle
The ankle is dislocated in three ways: externally, internally, and posteriorly, but not anteriorly.
The sign of dislocation in this patient is that when you command them to apply their knee with the cushion, you will know that it is dislocated.
The restoration of all the movements in this patient is that you should place the patient on their right side, so that they can extend their knee if possible; then bring your left hand to their face and place their foot between your thighs; then bring your palms together with force behind their thigh, and another servant should help pull their leg back into its correct position.
The sign of recovery in this patient is that their knee adheres to the cushion with effortless elevation
Then plaster that on and apply the bandage to hold the knee in place for three or four days, then remove it.
And do not allow the patient to walk around for a few days until they are healed and comfortable
But if you cannot reduce the dislocation yourself, then apply strong traction using the methods described in the previous narrative on treating hip dislocations.
Chapter XXXIII
On the treatment of ankle dislocation
The talus sometimes gets displaced by a slight movement, and sometimes it becomes fully dislocated, either by displacement inward or outward.
The sign of ankle dislocation is that the talus appears swollen at the part where it has been dislocated
But the treatment for ankle subluxation is easy reduction, by extending the foot with ease and then inverting it until it returns to its normal position
But when treating a fully displaced ankle dislocation, the following steps should be taken:
"You should then grasp the foot with your right hand from its highest point, and with your left hand on its lower side near the ankle joint."
"Then throw the leg over your right arm and hold it there with both hands while extending your left hand. Push the heel of the foot upwards with your right hand, placing the sole of the foot on a cushion without moving."
"And do this twice as we have mentioned."
"And next, press the sole of the foot onto the third bedpost, and you are at the ankle."
"If you cannot return to this manner in one or two attempts, and if the foot remains swollen, then repeat the operation again, because that one returns."
"But if you are prohibited from performing the reduction as mentioned above, then have the patient lie on their side on the ground. Take a sturdy stone and place it firmly between their thighs, and make sure they are covered with cloth so that the patient does not slip."
"And then the minister of the knee holds their things, and another minister extends the leg with its skin or bandages on the ankle lying on top of the foot's heel. And each minister extends their side against extension, ensuring that the stone is held tightly between the patient's thighs so that the body does not slip towards both sides during this extension."
"And then the doctor will reduce the dislocation with their hands, and another minister should hold the healthy leg on both sides to prevent any further movement during reduction."
"And then, once the dislocation is reduced and declared sound by you, then apply plaster and strengthen it with bandages. And then bind the foot with bandages to the thigh."
"And be careful and guard yourself from the nerves that are behind the ankle bone, because if a bandage is applied too tightly on them it may cause injury."
"And then leave him like this for two or three days. And if the bandage becomes loose, tighten it again."
"And then remove the splint on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days." "But if he is forced to walk before that time, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be dislodged and become corrupted again, which could prevent proper healing."
"And if an abscess forms due to the cauterization, then you must administer treatment as outlined in our previous narration regarding other locations for cure and embrocation until it is healed."
"Chapter XXXIV."
"On the treatment of dislocation of said feet."
"It is necessary that he who has been dislocated be stretched with little extension without working; and that which is not difficult, let it be easier beforehand."
"But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the foot, then you should make the patient place his foot on the ground or on a table, and let him be standing thus as if walking."
"And approaching, you stand on the affected foot, and pause his foot on whatever prominence there may be of the joints, and then press it with your foot with strength until it returns, so that you see it stretched out and does not appear in its prominence."
"And approach pausing the sole of the foot on a table that takes all the foot, and then stretch it with appropriate and firm strapping for three days; and afterward unbind him and make him rest from walking for several days until he is strengthened and recovery is assured."
"Chapter XXXV."
"Of the kinds of dislocation that are made with plague or fracture or with each one together."
When some cause of that happens, and you strive in its treatment and restoration, many times death occurs.
Therefore, it is not necessary to presume in its treatment, but rather that one who is skilled in the art, long experienced, merciful and compassionate, cautious, not hasty nor presumptuous; and that he begins by using medicines that remove blood clots alone, leaving the patient's fate to divine judgment, hoping for healing from harm, alleviation of illness, and showing some sign of hope. Then strive to reduce it at the onset of the cause before an abscess forms.
And if the member returns as desired, then administer treatment that will remove the abscess and heal the wound using appropriate drying agents.
And if dislocation with fracture occurs and the ends of the bone are separated, then strive to reduce them using what has been said previously regarding simple dislocations in those locations.
Guard and avoid, according to your ability, what is within the paths of fear as prescribed by my instruction; for that is better for you to owe to your Creator, more enduring for your glory, and healthier for your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Al-Bukasis perfectly, may God bless him. Amen.
